reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs a 3D transposed convolution, followed by two max pooling layers and a sum operation.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding)
        self.max_pool1 = nn.MaxPool3d(kernel_size=2)
        self.max_pool2 = nn.MaxPool3d(kernel_size=3)

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)
        x = self.max_pool1(x)
        x = self.max_pool2(x)
        x = torch.sum(x, dim=1, keepdim=True) 
        return x

batch_size = 16
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 64
depth, height, width = 32, 32, 32
kernel_size = 5
stride = 2
padding = 2

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding]

But ConvTranspose3d implementation effectively is a strided convolution of the input with kernel; compute cost is something like batch * in_channels * out_channels * kernel_size^3 * output spatial size? Actually transposed convolution compute: for each input element, we add kernel * input value to output region. That's large but manageable on GPU. Then maxpool1: trivial, memory bound. Maxpool2: trivial, memory bound. Sum: memory bound. Now, the fused approach: for each element of the final output [16, 1, 10, 10, 10], we need to compute: sum over c in [0, 63] of max_{i in region} (conv_transpose(x)[b, c, ...]) The conv_transpose is a linear operation: for each output channel c, position (d, h, w): conv_transpose(x)[b, c, d, h, w] = sum over in_ch, kd, kh, kw of weight[c, in_ch, kd, kh, kw] * x[b, in_ch, d', h', w'] where d' = (d - kd + padding) / stride, but only valid when (d - kd + padding) is divisible by stride... no wait. For ConvTranspose3d (also called deconvolution): output[b, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kd=0}^{K-1} sum_{kh=0}^{K-1} sum_{kw=0}^{K-1} weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] * x[b, c_in, (d + padding - kd)/stride, ...] More precisely, the input position is floor((d + padding - kd) / stride) when (d + padding - kd) is non-negative and divisible by stride? No, conv transpose is more complex. The standard formulation is: output[p] = sum_{q} weight[p - q * stride] * input[q] where p, q are spatial coordinates. For stride=2, each spatial position in the input contributes to K positions in the output (separated by stride), and these are summed where they overlap. Actually, the formula for ConvTranspose3d is: For each output spatial position (d_out, h_out, w_out): output[b, c_out, d_out, h_out, w_out] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kd} sum_{kh} sum_{kw} weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] * x[b, c_in, d', h', w'] where d' * stride + kd = d_out + padding, i.e., d' = (d_out + padding - kd) / stride And this only includes terms where (d_out + padding - kd) is divisible by stride AND d' is in valid range. For our case: stride=2, padding=2, K=5. d_out ranges 0..62. For a given output position d_out, the input positions that contribute are those d' where: d' = (d_out + 2 - kd) / 2 for some kd in [0, 4] and (d_out + 2 - kd) must be even and >= 0 and < 32 (input depth). So for a given output position d_out, there are up to ceil(5/2) = 3 input positions that contribute (since stride=2, about half the kernel positions map to valid input positions). OK, this is getting complicated for a fully fused kernel. Let me think about a two-kernel approach: 1. ConvTranspose3d using cuDNN (this is already highly optimized) 2. Fused kernel that does MaxPool1 + MaxPool2 + Sum Or even a three-kernel approach might be competitive if the conv transpose dominates. But wait - the conv transpose output is 16 * 64 * 63^3 ≈ 254M elements at fp32, which is about 1GB! This is memory-bound. The subsequent max pools and sum reduce this dramatically. So fusing the reductions is important but the conv transpose itself is the bottleneck. Actually, let me reconsider fusing the conv transpose with the max pools and sum. This would avoid writing the entire 1GB intermediate to memory. For the fully fused kernel, each thread would compute one element of the final output [16, 1, 10, 10, 10] = 16,000 elements. That's very small - we need much more parallelism. Actually we could compute each element of [16, 64, 10, 10, 10] and then sum across channels. Or better: parallelize over (b, c, d, h, w) where d, h, w are the final 10x10x10 spatial dims, giving 16 * 64 * 1000 = 1,024,000 parallel work items. Each thread computes: 1. For the reduction, with 64 threads we can do a tree reduction in shared memory. Or we can have each thread compute its channel's max value, store in shared memory, then reduce. Let me sketch the 256-thread version: ```cuda __global__ void fused_kernel(const float* in, float* out, int B, int C, int D1, int H1, int W1, int D2, int H2, int W2) { // Block processes 4 positions. Position index = blockIdx.x * 4 + (threadIdx.x / 64) int pos_idx = blockIdx.x * 4 + (threadIdx.x >> 6); // divide by 64 int c = threadIdx.x & 63; // mod 64 if (pos_idx >= B * D2 * H2 * W2) return; int total_spatial = D2 * H2 * W2; int b = pos_idx / total_spatial; int rem = pos_idx % total_spatial; int d2 = rem / (H2 * W2); rem %= (H2 * W2); int h2 = rem / W2; int w2 = rem % W2; // Compute max for this channel over the 6x6x6 region float max_val = -1e20f; const float* in_channel = in + ((b * C + c) * D1 + d2 * 6) * H1 * W1 + h2 * 6 * W1 + w2 * 6; // iterate dc, hc, wc in [0, 5] but check boundaries for (int dc = 0; dc < 6 && d2 * 6 + dc < D1; dc++) { for (int hc = 0; hc < 6 && h2 * 6 + hc < H1; hc++) { for (int wc = 0; wc < 6 && w2 * 6 + wc < W1; wc++) { float val = in_channel[dc * H1 * W1 + hc * W1 + wc]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } } // Reduce 64 channels __shared__ float sdata[256]; // or [4][64] int group = threadIdx.x >> 6; int lane = threadIdx.x & 63; sdata[group * 64 + lane] = max_val; __syncthreads(); // Tree sum for each group for (int stride = 32; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (lane < stride) { sdata[group * 64 + lane] += sdata[group * 64 + lane + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (lane == 0) { int out_idx = b * D2 * H2 * W2 + d2 * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2; out[out_idx] = sdata[group * 64]; } } ``` Wait, I'm using shared memory in a way that requires all threads in the block to participate. Since I have 4 groups of 64 threads and only 256 threads, this should work. But I need all threads to hit __syncthreads(), which they do if pos_idx is valid. For the last block, some positions might be invalid if B*D2*H2*W2 isn't divisible by 4. Actually B*D2*H2*W2 = 16*1000 = 16000, which is divisible by 4. Great! For the reduction, I need to be careful. Let me use a cleaner approach: ``` __shared__ float smem[4][64]; smem[group][lane] = max_val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = 32; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (lane < s) smem[group][lane] += smem[group][lane + s]; __syncthreads(); } if (lane == 0) out[...] = smem[group][0]; ``` This should work. wc < 6; wc++) {\n int w_pos = w2 * 6 + wc;\n if (w_pos >= W1) break;\n float v = base[dc * H1 * W1 + hc * W1 + wc];\n if (v > max_val) max_val = v;\n }\n }\n }\n }\n \n __shared__ float smem[256];\n smem[group * C + c] = max_val;\n __syncthreads();\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int stride = C / 2; For channel c at position (b, d, h, w), the memory address is: input[((b * C + c) * D + d) * H + h) * W + w] If consecutive threads read the same spatial position but different channels, they access addresses separated by D*H*W elements. With D=H=W=64, that's 262144 elements. At 4 bytes each, that's about 1MB apart. This is NOT coalesced! This is a major issue. For coalesced access, we want consecutive threads to access consecutive memory addresses. If thread 0 reads channel 0, position (0,0,0), its address is base + 0*D*H*W. If thread 1 reads channel 1, position (0,0,0), its address is base + 1*D*H*W. These are very far apart, so consecutive threads access wildly different memory locations. This leads to terrible cache behavior. For better memory access, I should have consecutive threads read the same channel but adjacent spatial positions, OR adjacent channels but the same spatial position if channels are stored interleaved. But in NCDHW format, channels are the outer dimension. For the current memory layout (NCDHW), the best approach for coalesced access is to have the inner loop over channels with the spatial position fixed, and have different warps/blocks handle different positions. Actually, for a 5D tensor with shape [B, C, D, H, W], if thread c accesses [b, c, d, h, w], the memory offset is: offset = ((b * C + c) * D + d) * H + h) * W + w = b * C * D * H * W + c * D * H * W + d * H * W + h * W + w Consecutive threads with consecutive c values access memory separated by D*H*W = 64^3 = 262144 floats = 1MB. So for threads in a warp to hit the same cache line, they'd need to differ by less than 64/C floats in the inner dimension, which isn't the case. This means each warp access is spread across many cache lines, leading to poor memory bandwidth utilization. To fix this, I need to restructure so that consecutive threads access consecutive memory. Shared memory usage is small but could be better organized Let me write a better kernel with warp-level cooperation for better coalescing: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D1, int H1, int W1 ) { // Each block handles 1 output position (b, d2, h2, w2) // Block has 512 threads = 16 warps // Each warp handles 4 channels (64/16=4) // Within a warp, 32 threads read spatial positions with consecutive W for coalescing int pos_idx = blockIdx.x; int D2 = (D1 + 5) / 6; // ceiling division for the output size int H2 = (H1 + 5) / 6; int W2 = (W1 + 5) / 6; if (pos_idx >= B * D2 * H2 * W2) return; int b = pos_idx / (D2 * H2 * W2); int rem = pos_idx % (D2 * H2 * W2); int d2 = rem / (H2 * W2); rem %= (H2 * W2); int h2 = rem / W2; int w2 = rem % W2; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int channels_per_warp = (C + blockDim.x / 32 - 1) / (blockDim.x / 32); int c_start = warp_id * channels_per_warp; int c_end = min(c_start + channels_per_warp, C); float warp_maxes[4]; // max 4 channels per warp int num_ch = c_end - c_start; for (int ci = 0; ci < num_ch; ci++) { int c = c_start + ci; int d_start = d2 * 6; int h_start = h2 * 6; int w_start = w2 * 6; float partial_max = -FLT_MAX; // Each thread reads spatial positions with stride 32 in W dimension for (int dd = 0; dd < 6; dd++) { int d = d_start + dd; if (d >= D1) continue; for (int dh = 0; dh < 6; dh++) { int h = h_start + dh; if (h >= H1) continue; // Handle W dimension with coalescing for (int dw = lane; dw < 6; dw += 32) { int w = w_start + dw; if (w >= W1) continue; // Compute address size_t idx = ((size_t)b * C * D1 * H1 * W1) + ((size_t)c * D1 * H1 * W1) + ((size_t)d * H1 * W1) + ((size_t)h * W1) + w; float val = input[idx]; if (val > partial_max) partial_max = val; } } } // Warp-level reduction for max #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial_max, offset); if (other > partial_max) partial_max = other; } warp_maxes[ci] = partial_max; } // Store warp maxes in shared memory __shared__ float sdata[64]; for (int ci = 0; ci < num_ch; ci++) { sdata[c_start + ci] = warp_maxes[ci]; } __syncthreads(); // Sum all channel maxes // Have one thread per block do the sum float sum_val = 0; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { sum_val += sdata[c]; } int out_idx = ((b * 1 + 0) * D2 + d2) * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2; output[out_idx] = sum_val; } } ``` Wait, there's a major issue with coalescing in this kernel too. Let me reconsider. For channel c, the memory layout is: base = b*C*D*H*W + c*D*H*W + d*H*W + h*W + w For a fixed c, d, h, consecutive w values are consecutive in memory. So if lane 0 reads w=w_start, lane 1 reads w=w_start+1, etc., that's coalesced. The issue is that my loop `for (int dw = lane; dw < 6; dw += 32)` means lane 0 reads w_start, w_start+32 (but 32 > 6, so only w_start). Since W only ranges from w_start to w_start+5 (at most 6 values), and we have 32 lanes, most lanes read nothing! This means for each (d, h), only lane 0..5 (or fewer) actually read data, and lanes 6..31 are idle. That's terrible utilization. I need to structure it differently. For a 6x6x6 = 216 element region, with 32 threads: - We can flatten the 3D region into 216 elements - Thread i reads elements i, i+32, i+64, ... up to 215 - This gives each thread about 7 elements to read For coalescing, consecutive threads should ideally read consecutive memory. But with 6x6x6 and W being the fastest dimension: index = d*H*W + h*W + w Consecutive w gives consecutive memory. But if thread i reads element i of the flattened 6x6x6, the memory access pattern depends on how we flatten. If we flatten as w fastest, then h, then d: flat_index = d*36 + h*6 + w Thread 0 reads element 0 = (d=0, h=0, w=0) -> memory address = base + 0 Thread 1 reads element 1 = (d=0, h=0, w=1) -> memory address = base + 1 ... Thread 5 reads element 5 = (d=0, h=0, w=5) -> memory address = base + 5 Thread 6 reads element 6 = (d=0, h=1, w=0) -> memory address = base + W (not base + 6) So threads 0-5 read consecutive memory, but thread 6 jumps by W-5 = 59 floats. This is only partially coalesced. For W=64, threads 0-5 read addresses base+0 to base+5 (coalesced, one cache line). Thread 6 reads base+64 (next cache line). Threads 6-11 read base+64 to base+69 (coalesced, one cache line). And so on. Within each group of 6 consecutive threads, accesses are coalesced. But there are gaps between groups. However, threads in a warp reading base+0..5 and base+64..69 might still access only 2 cache lines, which is efficient. Actually, for a warp of 32 threads reading elements 0..31: - Elements 0..5: addresses base+0..5 (cache line 1) - Elements 6..11: addresses base+64..69 (cache line 2) - Elements 12..17: addresses base+128..133 (cache line 3) - Elements 18..23: addresses base+192..197 (cache line 4) - Elements 24..29: addresses base+256..261 (cache line 5) - Elements 30..31: addresses base+320..321 (cache line 6) So a single warp read accesses 6 cache lines. Not ideal, but not terrible. For the next set (elements 32..63), it's similar but offset by 32 in the flat index. Element 32 = (d=0, h=5, w=2) -> base + 5*64 + 2 = base + 322 Element 33 = (d=0, h=5, w=3) -> base + 323 Actually, this pattern continues. So each warp of 32 threads accesses about 6 cache lines per read. Given that a cache line is 128 bytes (32 floats), reading 32 floats ideally takes 1 cache line. Here we take 6, so we have 6x cache line overhead. This is still much better than accessing 1MB apart! If I flatten [C, 6, 6, 6] as c*(6*6*6) + d*(6*6) + h*6 + w, then: - Element 0: c=0, d=0, h=0, w=0 - Element 1: c=0, d=0, h=0, w=1 - ... - Element 6: c=0, d=0, h=1, w=0 - Element 36: c=0, d=1, h=0, w=0 - Element 216: c=1, d=0, h=0, w=0 So consecutive elements in the flat array: - For elements 0..5: same c,d,h, varying w -> consecutive memory, COALESCED - Elements 6..35: same c,d, varying h,w -> w jumps by 1 but then jumps by W (64) when h increments -> mostly coalesced with a stride - Elements 36..215: same c, varying d,h,w -> when d increments, memory jumps by H*W (4096) - Elements 216..431: different c -> memory jumps by D*H*W (262144) So for threads 0..255 reading elements 0..255: - Threads 0..5 read w=0..5 for c=d=h=0 -> coalesced - Threads 6..35 read various (h,w) for c=d=0 -> stride pattern - Threads 36..215 read various (d,h,w) for c=0 -> larger strides - Threads 216..255 read w=0..39 for c=1, d=h=0 -> coalesced but different cache lines from earlier threads This is partially coalesced. Each group of threads accessing the same (c, d, h) reads consecutive W values and gets coalesced. But groups accessing different (c, d, h) go to different cache lines. A single warp of 32 threads might access elements 0..31, which covers: - w=0..5 for c=d=h=0 (6 elements) - h=1, w=0..5 for c=d=0 (6 elements) - h=2, w=0..5 (6 elements) - h=3, w=0..5 (6 elements) - h=4, w=0..5 (6 elements) - h=5, w=0..1 (2 elements) These memory addresses are: base + 0, base+1, ..., base+5, base + 64, base+65, ..., base+69, base + 128, ..., base+133, base + 192, ..., base+197, base + 256, ..., base+261, base + 320, base+321 So a warp accesses 6 different cache lines (each group of 6 consecutive elements in a cache line, but with 64-float gaps). This is actually handled well by modern GPUs - 6 cache line loads per warp is acceptable. If we continue this pattern for the full 256 threads (8 warps), we'd access more cache lines. But since H100 has high memory bandwidth and coalescing hardware, this should be reasonably efficient. After loading into shared memory, we can restructure the computation. Each thread (or warp) handles max reduction for some channels. Actually, I just realized: if we use shared memory to cache the 6x6x6 region, we don't need coalesced access for the max computation, only for the initial load. And the load pattern is partially coalesced. For the max computation: - We have smem[c][d][h][w] for c=0..63, d=0..5, h=0..5, w=0..5 - Each thread computes max over the 216 values for some channels - For example, if we have 64 threads, thread c computes max over smem[c][*][*][*] - This is a sequential scan over shared memory, very fast Then for the sum over channels: - We can do a tree reduction in shared memory - 64 threads, each adds their channel max, then reduce This seems like a great approach! Let me write it: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D1, int H1, int W1 ) { const int D2 = (D1 + 5) / 6; const int H2 = (H1 + 5) / 6; const int W2 = (W1 + 5) / 6; int pos_idx = blockIdx.x; if (pos_idx >= B * D2 * H2 * W2) return; int b = pos_idx / (D2 * H2 * W2); int rem = pos_idx % (D2 * H2 * W2); int d2 = rem / (H2 * W2); rem %= (H2 * W2); int h2 = rem / W2; int w2 = rem % W2; int d_base = d2 * 6; int h_base = h2 * 6; int w_base = w2 * 6; // Shared memory: C * 6 * 6 * 6 floats __shared__ float smem[64 * 216]; // Load data into shared memory // Total elements = C * 6 * 6 * 6 = 13824 // Threads in block = 256 // Each thread loads about 54 elements int total_elems = C * 216; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elems; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / 216; int rem = idx % 216; int d_off = rem / 36; rem %= 36; int h_off = rem / 6; int w_off = rem % 6; int d = d_base + d_off; int h = h_base + h_off; int w = w_base + w_off; float val = 0; if (d < D1 && h < H1 && w < W1) { size_t in_idx = ((size_t)b * C * D1 * H1 * W1) + ((size_t)c * D1 * H1 * W1) + ((size_t)d * H1 * W1) + ((size_t)h * W1) + w; val = input[in_idx]; } smem[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread computes max for some channels float thread_max = 0; int channels_per_thread = (C + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; int c_start = threadIdx.x * channels_per_thread; int c_end = min(c_start + channels_per_thread, C); for (int c = c_start; c < c_end; c++) { float max_val = -FLT_MAX; for (int i = 0; i < 216; i++) { float v = smem[c * 216 + i]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } thread_max += max_val; // accumulate sum of maxes } // Tree reduction for sum __shared__ float sdata[256]; sdata[threadIdx.x] = thread_max; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int out_idx = ((b * 1 + 0) * D2 + d2) * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2; output[out_idx] = sdata[0]; } } ``` Wait, this has an issue. If blockDim.x = 256 and C = 64, then channels_per_thread = 1/4, which means many threads handle 0 channels. That's wasteful. Let me fix: instead of (C + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x, I should compute how many threads actually need to work. If C = 64 and blockDim.x = 256, only 64 threads need to do the max computation. The other 192 threads are idle during the max phase but participate in the final reduction. That's wrong - they'd contribute 0 to the sum, which is fine, but it's wasteful. Better: have exactly C threads do the channel maxes, then do a C-thread reduction. ```cpp // Only first C threads compute channel maxes float channel_max = 0; if (threadIdx.x < C) { int c = threadIdx.x; float max_val = -FLT_MAX; for (int i = 0; i < 216; i++) { float v = smem[c * 216 + i]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } channel_max = max_val; } // Store in shared memory for reduction __shared__ float sdata[64]; // only need C entries if (threadIdx.x < C) { sdata[threadIdx.x] = channel_max; } __syncthreads(); // Reduce C values if (threadIdx.x < 32) { // one warp float sum = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += 32) { sum += sdata[i]; } // Warp-level reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int out_idx = ((b * 1 + 0) * D2 + d2) * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2; output[out_idx] = sum; } } ``` This is much better! Only 64 threads do the max computation, then 32 threads do the warp-level sum. The other threads in the block (256 - 64 = 192 threads) are idle after loading shared memory. But that's OK because loading is where most work is. Wait, with only 64 threads doing the max, the loop over 216 elements per channel is sequential for each thread. That's 216 * 64 = 13,824 iterations total. With 64 threads, that's fine - each thread does 216 iterations. But the accesses to shared memory are all close together (different threads access different channels), which might cause bank conflicts. In shared memory, smem[c * 216 + i] for different c values but same i: - Thread 0 accesses smem[0 * 216 + i] = smem[i] - Thread 1 accesses smem[1 * 216 + i] = smem[i + 216] - These are 216 floats apart = 864 bytes On modern GPUs, shared memory has 32 banks, and each bank is 4 bytes. 864 / (32 * 4) = 864 / 128 = 6.75 banks apart. So different threads access different banks, which avoids bank conflicts. Good! During the load phase, thread t loads elements t, t+256, t+512, etc. These are not necessarily consecutive. Is there a mathematical simplification of conv_transpose + maxpool + sum? Conv_transpose(x)[b, c, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} x[b, c_in, d_in, h_in, w_in] * W[c, c_in, kd, kh, kw] where d = d_in * 2 + kd - 2 (because padding=2, stride=2) Maxpool1 with kernel=2 takes max over [d, d+1] x [h, h+1] x [w, w+1]. Maxpool2 with kernel=3 takes max over [d, d+2] x [h, h+2] x [w, w+2]. So combined, maxpool1 then maxpool2 is equivalent to a single maxpool with kernel=6, stride=6. Output at (d_out, h_out, w_out) = max over [6*d_out, 6*d_out+5] x [6*h_out, 6*h_out+5] x [6*w_out, 6*w_out+5]. And then sum over channels. So: output[b, 0, d_out, h_out, w_out] = sum_c max_{dd,dh,dw in [0,6)^3} conv_transpose(b, c, 6*d_out+dd, 6*h_out+dh, 6*w_out+dw) Expanding conv_transpose: = sum_c max_{dd,dh,dw} sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} x[b, c_in, (6*d_out+dd+2-kd)/2, ...] * W[c, c_in, kd, kh, kw] For this to be valid, 6*d_out+dd+2-kd must be even and non-negative. Since dd ranges 0..5 and kd ranges 0..4: dd + 2 - kd ranges from 0+2-4 = -2 to 5+2-0 = 7. For the expression to be even: dd + 2 - kd ≡ 0 (mod 2) dd - kd ≡ 0 (mod 2) dd ≡ kd (mod 2) So for each dd, only kd with the same parity contributes. For a fixed output position (d_out, h_out, w_out) and fixed dd, dh, dw: The valid (kd, kh, kw) are those where kd ≡ dd (mod 2), kh ≡ dh (mod 2), kw ≡ dw (mod 2). The conv_transpose output at (6*d_out+dd, 6*h_out+dh, 6*w_out+dw) is: sum_{c_in} sum_{kd ≡ dd} sum_{kh ≡ dh} sum_{kw ≡ dw} x[b, c_in, d_in, h_in, w_in] * W[c, c_in, kd, kh, kw] where d_in = (6*d_out + dd + 2 - kd) / 2 Now, max over dd, dh, dw of this expression, then sum over c. This is: sum_c max_{dd,dh,dw} sum_{c_in} sum_{kd ≡ dd} sum_{kh ≡ dh} sum_{kw ≡ dw} x[b, c_in, ...] * W[c, c_in, kd, kh, kw] For each parity combination of (dd, dh, dw), there are 3*3*3=27 or 2*2*2=8 valid (kd, kh, kw) combinations. Since max and sum don't commute, we can't simplify this much further. However, note that the max is over the 6x6x6 region, and for each spatial position in that region, the conv_transpose value is a linear combination of input values and weights. The max of linear combinations is NOT a linear combination, so we can't simplify. This means fusing conv_transpose with maxpool requires computing all conv_transpose values for the 6x6x6 region and then taking max. This is computationally expensive unless we can cache or reuse computations. One possibility: precompute the input positions that contribute to each output spatial position in the 6x6x6 region. For each (dd, dh, dw), the input positions are determined by (kd, kh, kw). The input depths are in range: d_in = (6*d_out + dd + 2 - kd) / 2 For d_out=0, dd=0..5, kd=0..4 with kd≡dd mod 2: - dd=0, kd=0,2,4: d_in = (0+0+2-0)/2=1, (0+0+2-2)/2=0, (0+0+2-4)/2=-1(invalid) So d_in = 1, 0 for valid ones. Actually (2-4)/2=-1 is invalid. Hmm, this is getting complicated. Let me think about whether there's an efficient way to structure this computation. For a fixed (b, d_out, h_out, w_out): The output depends on input x[b, :, d_in_range, h_in_range, w_in_range]. For d_out=0, the max region covers conv_transpose positions d=0..5. For each d, the input depths are: d=0: kd=0->d_in=1, kd=2->d_in=0, kd=4->invalid d=1: kd=1->d_in=1, kd=3->d_in=0 d=2: kd=0->d_in=2, kd=2->d_in=1, kd=4->d_in=0 d=3: kd=1->d_in=2, kd=3->d_in=1 d=4: kd=0->d_in=3, kd=2->d_in=2, kd=4->d_in=1 d=5: kd=1->d_in=3, kd=3->d_in=2 So input depths involved are 0,1,2,3. I want to make sure: 1. The shared memory approach works 2. I handle the output sizes correctly 3. The reduction is efficient Let me draft the CUDA code: ```cpp __global__ void fused_maxpool_sum_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D1, int H1, int W1 ) { const int D2 = (D1 + 5) / 6; const int H2 = (H1 + 5) / 6; const int W2 = (W1 + 5) / 6; int pos_idx = blockIdx.x; if (pos_idx >= B * D2 * H2 * W2) return; int b = pos_idx / (D2 * H2 * W2); int rem = pos_idx % (D2 * H2 * W2); int d2 = rem / (H2 * W2); rem %= (H2 * W2); int h2 = rem / W2; int w2 = rem % W2; int d_base = d2 * 6; int h_base = h2 * 6; int w_base = w2 * 6; // Shared memory for the 6x6x6 region of all channels // Size: C * 6 * 6 * 6 floats const int SPATIAL = 216; __shared__ float smem[64 * SPATIAL]; // Load data cooperatively // Total elements to load = C * SPATIAL int total = C * SPATIAL; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / SPATIAL; int s = idx % SPATIAL; int d_off = s / 36; int rem = s % 36; int h_off = rem / 6; int w_off = rem % 6; int d = d_base + d_off; int h = h_base + h_off; int w = w_base + w_off; float val = -FLT_MAX; if (d < D1 && h < H1 && w < W1) { size_t in_idx = ((size_t)b * C + c) * D1 * H1 * W1 + ((size_t)d * H1 + h) * W1 + w; val = input[in_idx]; } smem[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute max for each channel __shared__ float channel_max[64]; int channels_per_thread = (C + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; int c_start = threadIdx.x * channels_per_thread; int c_end = min(c_start + channels_per_thread, C); float my_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = c_start; c < c_end; c++) { float max_val = -FLT_MAX; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < SPATIAL; i++) { float v = smem[c * SPATIAL + i]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } my_sum += max_val; } // Reduction over threads using shared memory __shared__ float sdata[256]; sdata[threadIdx.x] = my_sum; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int out_idx = ((b * 1 + 0) * D2 + d2) * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2; output[out_idx] = sdata[0]; } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with the channel max reduction. If C=64 and blockDim.x=256, then channels_per_thread = 1/4 = 0 (integer division). Actually: channels_per_thread = (64 + 256 - 1) / 256 = 319 / 256 = 1. So c_start = threadIdx.x * 1 = threadIdx.x, and c_end = min(threadIdx.x + 1, 64). This means threads 0..63 each handle 1 channel, and threads 64..255 handle 0 channels. my_sum for threads 64..255 = 0. The reduction adds all 256 values, but only the first 64 are non-zero. That's correct, but wasteful. Better to have only 64 threads do the work and then a 64-thread reduction: ```cpp float my_max = 0.0f; if (threadIdx.x < C) { int c = threadIdx.x; float max_val = -FLT_MAX; for (int i = 0; i < SPATIAL; i++) { float v = smem[c * SPATIAL + i]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } my_max = max_val; } // Store in shared memory for reduction __shared__ float sdata[256]; sdata[threadIdx.x] = my_max; __syncthreads(); // Reduce first C elements if (threadIdx.x < 128) { if (threadIdx.x + 128 < C) sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + 128]; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < 64) { if (threadIdx.x + 64 < C) sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + 64]; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < 32) { float val = sdata[threadIdx.x]; // Warp shuffle reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { output[out_idx] = val; } } ``` Hmm, but the conditional additions are messy with variable C. Also, the tree reduction with branches might be slower. For C=64 specifically, I can hardcode a 64-thread reduction: ```cpp if (threadIdx.x < 64) { float val = sdata[threadIdx.x]; // 64 -> 32 val += sdata[threadIdx.x + 32]; // warp reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { output[out_idx] = val; } } ``` This requires that the first 64 threads load their values and all other threads have 0 in sdata. Then thread 0..31 each add sdata[idx] + sdata[idx+32]. For idx=0: adds sdata[0] + sdata[32] For idx=1: adds sdata[1] + sdata[33] ... For idx=31: adds sdata[31] + sdata[63] Then warp-level shuffle reduction sums these 32 values. This works if C <= 64. If C > 64, I'd need a different approach. But in this problem, C = out_channels = 64. Let me verify: in the reference, `out_channels = 64`. So I can hardcode C=64. Actually, better to make it work for any C as a general kernel, but optimize for the common case. However, hardcoding 64 might make the kernel slightly faster due to fewer branches. That's an 8-way bank conflict! Very bad for shared memory performance. I need to pad the shared memory to avoid bank conflicts. Instead of SPATIAL=216, use SPATIAL_PADDED=224 (next multiple of 32, since 224 % 32 = 0, so 224/32 = 7, and 7*4 = 28... wait). If I pad to 224: Bank = (i * 224) % 32 224 % 32 = 0 Bank = 0 for all i! That's even worse - all threads hit the same bank! I need to choose a padding where consecutive accesses hit different banks. For shared memory access smem[i * PADDED]: Bank = (i * PADDED) % 32 I want the mapping i -> (i * PADDED) % 32 to be a permutation for i=0..31. This requires gcd(PADDED, 32) = 1, i.e., PADDED must be odd. If PADDED is odd, then i*PADDED mod 32 cycles through all values as i goes 0..31. 216 is even, so gcd(216, 32) = 8. That's why we got 8-way conflicts. If I add 1 to get 217: 217 % 32 = 25 gcd(217, 32) = 1 since 217 is odd. So bank = (i * 25) % 32 gives all 32 banks for i=0..31. No conflicts! Let me write this version: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel(...) { // ... compute b, d2, h2, w2, d_base, h_base, w_base int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; float channel_max = 0.0f; // Each warp handles channels: warp_id, warp_id+num_warps, ... for (int c = warp_id; c < C; c += num_warps) { float local_max = -FLT_MAX; for (int idx = lane; idx < 216; idx += 32) { int d_off = idx / 36; int srem = idx % 36; int h_off = srem / 6; int w_off = srem % 6; int d = d_base + d_off; int h = h_base + h_off; int w = w_base + w_off; if (d < D1 && h < H1 && w < W1) { size_t in_idx = ((size_t)b * C + c) * D1 * H1 * W1 + ((size_t)d * H1 + h) * W1 + w; float val = input[in_idx]; if (val > local_max) local_max = val; } } // Warp-level max reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset); if (other > local_max) local_max = other; } if (lane == 0) { // Atomically or use shared memory to accumulate // ... } } } ``` For accumulating the channel maxes, I can use shared memory: ```cpp __shared__ float warp_maxes[8]; // 8 warps if (lane == 0) { warp_maxes[warp_id] = local_max; } __syncthreads(); // Reduce warp maxes if (threadIdx.x < 8) { // or use warp 0 float val = (threadIdx.x < num_warps) ? warp_maxes[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 4; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFF, val, offset); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { output[out_idx] = val; } } ``` This approach: - No need for large shared memory (only 8 floats) - Each warp independently loads and reduces for its channels - Only one __syncthreads() for the cross-warp reduction The downside is that consecutive warps access different channels at the same time (1MB apart), which might cause cache thrashing. For a single warp reading 216 elements of one channel: The elements span memory addresses from base + 0 to base + 321 (for the first 32 elements), then base + 322 to base + 640, etc. This spans about 640 floats = 2.5KB per warp per channel. But different warps in the same block read different channels, which are 1MB apart. These won't share L1 cache lines. L2 is 50MB+, so all 64 channels = 55KB easily fits. If warps execute sequentially (one channel at a time), each channel's data is fetched from L2 to L1, used, and then evicted. The next channel is fetched. This happens 8 times per block. For 16000 blocks, that's 128,000 fetches. L2 handles this fine. So the main bottleneck is HBM bandwidth for initial loads, and then L2/L1 for repeated access. Since each channel's data is only read once, the HBM bandwidth dominates. Total HBM reads: ~844MB. Here's my planned kernel: - Block size: 256 threads - Shared memory: 64 * 217 floats = 55,552 bytes (with padding to avoid bank conflicts) - Each block handles 1 output position - Threads load total elements (C * 216) in a loop with stride blockDim.x - After __syncthreads(), threads 0..C-1 compute max for their channel - Then tree reduction over C values For bank-conflict-free max computation, I'll store: `smem[c * SPATIAL_PADDED + i]` where SPATIAL_PADDED = 220 (next multiple of 32 after 216? No, I need GCD(SPATIAL_PADDED, 32) = 1). Actually wait, let me reconsider. During max computation, threads 0..63 read: `smem[c * PADDED + i]` for c=0..63 and i varying from 0 to 215. For a fixed i, consecutive c values access: `smem[0*PADDED + i], smem[1*PADDED + i], smem[2*PADDED + i], ...` These are at offsets i, PADDED+i, 2*PADDED+i, ... Bank for offset j is (j / 4) % 32 (since each float is 4 bytes). So bank for c is: ((c * PADDED + i) / 4) % 32 For c=0: (i/4) % 32 c=1: ((PADDED + i) / 4) % 32 We want these to be different for all c in a warp (c=0..31). This requires that ((PADDED) / 4) % 32 generates a full cycle. If PADDED/4 is odd, then multiplying by c and taking mod 32 gives all values. If PADDED/4 is even, we get conflicts. So I need PADDED/4 to be odd, which means PADDED must be divisible by 4 but not by 8. Or more generally, PADDED/4 and 32 must be coprime. 32 = 2^5. So PADDED/4 must be odd. PADDED must be of the form 4 * odd. Examples: PADDED = 4: 4/4 = 1 (odd) -> works, but too small PADDED = 12: 12/4 = 3 (odd) -> works PADDED = 20: 20/4 = 5 (odd) -> works PADDED = 28: 28/4 = 7 (odd) -> works PADDED = 36: 36/4 = 9 (odd) -> works I need PADDED >= 216. 216/4 = 54 (even). So PADDED=216 doesn't work. 220/4 = 55 (odd). PADDED=220 works! 228/4 = 57 (odd). PADDED=228 works! Let me use PADDED = 220. This wastes 4 floats per channel = 256 bytes total. Total shared memory = 64 * 220 * 4 = 56,320 bytes. Actually, let me double check for PADDED=220: c=0 accesses offset i, bank = (i/4) % 32 c=1 accesses offset 220+i, bank = ((220+i)/4) % 32 = (55 + i/4) % 32 Since 55 is odd, adding 55 mod 32 = 23 (which is odd). So c=1's bank is (i/4 + 23) % 32, which is different from c=0's bank for all i. For c=2: bank = (110 + i/4) % 32 = (22 + i/4) % 32. Different from c=0 and c=1. In fact, c*k gives bank offset k*23 mod 32. Since gcd(23, 32)=1, this cycles through all 32 banks. Great, PADDED=220 works. But wait, what about the warp reduction for the sum? OK, here's the final kernel design: ```cpp __global__ void fused_maxpool_sum_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D1, int H1, int W1 ) { const int D2 = (D1 / 2 - 3) / 3 + 1; const int H2 = (H1 / 2 - 3) / 3 + 1; const int W2 = (W1 / 2 - 3) / 3 + 1; const int SPATIAL = 216; const int SPATIAL_PADDED = 220; int pos_idx = blockIdx.x; if (pos_idx >= B * D2 * H2 * W2) return; int b = pos_idx / (D2 * H2 * W2); int rem = pos_idx % (D2 * H2 * W2); int d2 = rem / (H2 * W2); rem %= (H2 * W2); int h2 = rem / W2; int w2 = rem % W2; int d_base = d2 * 6; int h_base = h2 * 6; int w_base = w2 * 6; __shared__ float smem[64 * SPATIAL_PADDED]; __shared__ float max_buf[64]; // Load all channels and spatial positions int total = C * SPATIAL; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { int c = idx / SPATIAL; int s = idx % SPATIAL; int d_off = s / 36; int srem = s % 36; int h_off = srem / 6; int w_off = srem % 6; int d = d_base + d_off; int h = h_base + h_off; int w = w_base + w_off; float val = -FLT_MAX; if (d < D1 && h < H1 && w < W1) { size_t in_idx = ((size_t)b * C + c) * D1 * H1 * W1 + ((size_t)d * H1 + h) * W1 + w; val = input[in_idx]; } smem[c * SPATIAL_PADDED + s] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute max for each channel if (threadIdx.x < C) { int c = threadIdx.x; float max_val = -FLT_MAX; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < SPATIAL; i++) { float v = smem[c * SPATIAL_PADDED + i]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } max_buf[c] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); // Reduce over channels if (threadIdx.x < 32) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += 32) { sum += max_buf[i]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int out_idx = ((b * 1 + 0) * D2 + d2) * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2; output[out_idx] = sum; } } } ``` Wait, I need to double-check the formula for D2. Let me just write the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_maxpool_sum_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D1, int H1, int W1 ) { // Compute output sizes int D1a = D1 / 2; // after first maxpool(2) int H1a = H1 / 2; int W1a = W1 / 2; int D2 = (D1a - 3) / 3 + 1; // after second maxpool(3) int H2 = (H1a - 3) / 3 + 1; int W2 = (W1a - 3) / 3 + 1; int total_out = B * D2 * H2 * W2; int pos_idx = blockIdx.x; if (pos_idx >= total_out) return; int b = pos_idx / (D2 * H2 * W2); int rem = pos_idx % (D2 * H2 * W2); int d2 = rem / (H2 * W2); rem %= (H2 * W2); int h2 = rem / W2; int w2 = rem % W2; int c = threadIdx.x; if (c >= C) return; int d_base = d2 * 6; int h_base = h2 * 6; int w_base = w2 * 6; float max_val = -FLT_MAX; for (int dc = 0; dc < 6; ++dc) { int d = d_base + dc; if (d >= D1) break; for (int hc = 0; hc < 6; ++hc) { int h = h_base + hc; if (h >= H1) break; size_t offset = ((size_t)(b * C + c) * D1 + d) * H1 + h) * W1 + w_base; // Need to read w_base..w_base+5 for (int wc = 0; wc < 6; ++wc) { int w = w_base + wc; if (w >= W1) break; float v = input[offset + wc]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } } } __shared__ float smem[64]; smem[c] = max_val; __syncthreads(); // Reduce over channels if (c < 32) { float sum = smem[c] + smem[c + 32]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } if (c == 0) { int out_idx = ((b * 1 + 0) * D2 + d2) * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2; output[out_idx] = sum; } } } ``` Wait, I need to be careful with the indexing. Let me trace through it: For channel c, the base address for (d, h, w_base) is: base = input + ((b * C + c) * D1 + d) * H1 * W1 + h * W1 + w_base But input is a 1D pointer. The original tensor is [B, C, D, H, W]. Linear index of (b, c, d, h, w) = ((b * C + c) * D + d) * H * W + h * W + w Yes, that's correct. Let me simplify the inner loop: ```cuda size_t base_c = ((size_t)b * C + c) * D1 * H1 * W1; for (int dc = 0; dc < 6; ++dc) { int d = d_base + dc; if (d >= D1) break; size_t base_d = base_c + (size_t)d * H1 * W1; for (int hc = 0; hc < 6; ++hc) { int h = h_base + hc; if (h >= H1) break; const float* ptr = input + base_d + h * W1 + w_base; #pragma unroll for (int wc = 0; wc < 6; ++wc) { if (w_base + wc >= W1) break; float v = ptr[wc]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } } } ``` This avoids recomputing the offset each time. Now, the compiler will try to optimize this. The #pragma unroll for wc will unroll the 6-iteration loop. The compiler might also vectorize or optimize the memory loads. offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset);\n }\n if (c == 0) {\n int out_idx = ((b * 1 + 0) * D2 + d2) * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2;\n output[out_idx] = sum;\n }\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor fused_maxpool_sum(torch::Tensor input) {\n int B = input.size(0);\n int C = input.size(1);\n int D1 = input.size(2);\n int H1 = input.size(3);\n int W1 = input.size(4);\n \n int D1a = D1 / 2;\n int H1a = H1 / 2;\n int W1a = W1 / 2;\n int D2 = (D1a - 3) / 3 + 1;\n int H2 = (H1a - 3) / 3 + 1;\n int W2 = (W1a - 3) / 3 + 1;\n \n auto output = torch::empty({B, 1, D2, H2, W2}, input.options());\n int total_out = B * D2 * H2 * W2;\n \n fused_maxpool_sum_kernel<<<total_out, C>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, C, D1, H1, W1\n );\n \n return output;\n}\n\"\"\"\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name='fused_maxpool_sum_module_v3',\n cpp_sources=cpp_sources,\n cuda_sources=cuda_sources,\n functions=['fused_maxpool_sum'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],\n verbose=False\n)\n\nfused_maxpool_sum = module.fused_maxpool_sum\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding)\n \n def forward(self, x):\n x = self.conv_transpose(x)\n x = fused_maxpool_sum(x)\n return x\n" }

reasoning
The kernel passes correctness. Let me think about how to optimize the performance. The kernel is doing the maxpool and sum in one go. But there's one big issue: we are launching 16 * 10 * 10 * 10 = 16,000 blocks, each with 64 threads. Since each block only has 64 threads, the occupancy might be limited. Also, the kernel is memory-bound. Each block loads 64 channels * 6 * 6 * 6 = 13824 floats from different parts of memory. This is a lot of memory traffic. The conv transpose output is 64 * 65 * 65 * 65 which is about 11 MB. So the overall memory bandwidth required is not huge. However, the kernel is not very efficient because: 1. Each block only has 64 threads 2. Threads in a warp are handling different channels, and since they access the same memory locations (same xyz, different channels), they are accessing sequential memory. Wait, actually, the input layout is (N, C, D, H, W), so for a fixed batch, channel is the slowest dimension to change. When we process channels by threadIdx.x, threads within a warp are accessing memory with stride D*H*W elements apart. This is terrible for memory coalescing. Actually, let me reconsider: the input is in NCDHW format. For a given spatial position (d, h, w), the memory for channel c is at: `base_c + (d*H + h)*W + w` where `base_c = (b*C + c)*D*H*W`. So threads 0,1,2 in the same warp access memory with stride D*H*W = 65*65*65 = 274625 floats apart = 1.1MB apart. This is extremely non-coalesced. For 64 threads in a warp, they access 64 different channels. This means each memory access by a warp is scattered across 64MB of memory! This is the worst possible access pattern. We need to change the threading to have threads within a warp access consecutive memory (same channel, different spatial positions). Let me redesign: Our goal for each output pixel: find max over a 6x6x6 window, then sum over 64 channels. Better approach: - Have each block compute one spatial position (one output element) - Have enough threads (256) to process the data - We can process channel groups iteratively Since we have 64 channels and 216 spatial positions, we could: - Have each thread load one or more (c, spatial_pos) pairs - Store in shared memory, then compute max per channel and sum But shared memory is limited. For 64 channels * 216 floats = ~55KB. That's within 48KB on H100. Not on some GPUs but H100 has 228KB per SM so 55KB is fine. Actually wait, 64 * 216 * 4 = 55,296 bytes. H100 SM allows up to 227KB, so this is fine. Let's redesign with shared memory: 1. Each block computes one output position (b, d2, h2, w2) 2. Load the 6x6x6 x C region to shared memory cooperatively by threads 3. Each thread takes a few elements 4. After loading, compute max per channel, then sum across channels To improve coalescing, have adjacent threads load adjacent elements in the memory. Since NCDHW means adjacent elements in a channel are spatially adjacent, we can have thread i load consecutive floats from memory. But we have 64 channels * 216 spatial positions = 13824 elements. With 256 threads, that's about 54 elements per thread. Actually, let me think differently. The memory access pattern for reading NCDHW format is: - (b, 0, d, h, w), (b, 1, d, h, w), ..., (b, 63, d, h, w) are separated by D*H*W - (b, c, d, h, w) and (b, c, d, h, w+1) are adjacent For coalescing, consecutive threads should access consecutive memory. So if thread 0 reads (b, c, d, h, w), thread 1 should read (b, c, d, h, w+1). But then computing the max per channel requires reducing over spatial positions for each channel. Let me try: - Have blocks process multiple output positions - Each warp processes one output position for one channel at a time - Within a warp, threads process different spatial elements in parallel - Use warp shuffle to reduce max over spatial positions This approach: 1. Each warp = 32 threads 2. Each thread in warp loads ~7 spatial positions (from 1 channel) 3. Compute max using warp shuffle 4. Then sum across channels But we have 64 channels. We could have 2 warps process one position for 2 channels at a time, or 32 warps process 32 channels at a time. Actually: - One block has 256 threads = 8 warps - Process 1 output position - Each warp handles 8 channels (8 warps * 8 channels = 64) - Within each warp, each thread handles ~27 spatial elements This gives good coalescing because: - Warp 0 processes channel 0: threads load elements (0, 0..31), then (0, 32..63), etc. Spatial elements are adjacent in memory. - Warp 1 processes channel 1: similarly coalesced This is much better. Let me write this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_kernel(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C, int D1, int H1, int W1) { int D2 = (D1/2 - 3)/3 + 1; int H2 = (H1/2 - 3)/3 + 1; int W2 = (W1/2 - 3)/3 + 1; int pos = blockIdx.x; int b = pos / (D2 * H2 * W2); int rem = pos % (D2 * H2 * W2); int d2 = rem / (H2 * W2); rem %= H2 * W2; int h2 = rem / W2; int w2 = rem % W2; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; int channels_per_warp = 8; // 64 channels / 8 warps int c_start = warp_id * channels_per_warp; int d_base = d2 * 6, h_base = h2 * 6, w_base = w2 * 6; float max_vals[8]; // max for each of my channels for (int i = 0; i < channels_per_warp; i++) max_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX; size_t spatial_stride = (size_t)D1 * H1 * W1; for (int c_idx = 0; c_idx < channels_per_warp; c_idx++) { int c = c_start + c_idx; size_t base_channel = ((size_t)b * C + c) * spatial_stride; int spatial_idx = lane_id; while (spatial_idx < 216) { int dc = spatial_idx / 36; int rem_s = spatial_idx % 36; int hc = rem_s / 6; int wc = rem_s % 6; int d = d_base + dc; int h = h_base + hc; int w = w_base + wc; float val = -FLT_MAX; if (d < D1 && h < H1 && w < W1) { val = in[base_channel + ((size_t)d * H1 + h) * W1 + w]; } if (val > max_vals[c_idx]) max_vals[c_idx] = val; spatial_idx += 32; } } // Warp-level reduction for each channel #pragma unroll for (int c_idx = 0; c_idx < channels_per_warp; c_idx++) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_vals[c_idx], offset); if (other > max_vals[c_idx]) max_vals[c_idx] = other; } } // Now write maxes to shared memory and compute sum __shared__ float smem[64]; if (lane_id == 0) { #pragma unroll for (int c_idx = 0; c_idx < channels_per_warp; c_idx++) { smem[c_start + c_idx] = max_vals[c_idx]; } } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < 32) { float sum = 0; #pragma unroll for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += 32) { sum += smem[i]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { out[((b * 1 + 0) * D2 + d2) * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2] = sum; } } } ``` Wait, but each warp processes 8 channels, and within a warp each thread iterates over spatial positions. Since all threads in the warp process the same 8 channels but different spatial positions, and since channels are far apart, within one iteration all threads might access completely different channels... no wait. The problem is in the while loop. For `c_idx = 0` and `lane_id = 0`, thread accesses channel `c = c_start` spatial position 0. For `c_idx = 0` and `lane_id = 1`, thread accesses channel `c = c_start` spatial position 1. These two access adjacent memory positions (because they're in the same channel but adjacent spatial positions). This is perfectly coalesced! For `c_idx = 1` and `lane_id = 0`, thread accesses channel `c = c_start + 1` spatial position 0. This is at spatial_stride away from the first. Wait, but warp executes in lockstep for branches. In the outer for loop `for (c_idx...)`, all threads execute sequentially. So iteration c_idx=0: all warp threads access channel c_start (different spatial positions - coalesced). Iteration c_idx=1: all warp threads access channel c_start+1 (different spatial positions - coalesced). This is actually ideal! Each iteration accesses one channel with fully coalesced memory. However, there's a problem: the same warp is doing 8 iterations, each accessing a different channel. Each channel requires loading 216 floats. A warp has 32 threads, so it loads 32 floats at a time. Each load to a new channel might be a cache miss since channels are 1MB apart. But we can hope the L2 cache helps. Actually, for 64 channels, each output element needs loads from 64 * 216 = ~14K floats. For 16K output elements, total memory reads = 16K * 14K = 224M floats = ~900MB. This is not that much, so cache might help. But there's a bigger problem: block_size = 256, grid_size = 16K blocks, so total threads = 4M. Each block processes only 1 output element. With 132 SMs, active blocks per SM would be a lot but occupancy might be limited. Actually, 16K blocks with 256 threads is 4M threads. H100 has 14592 CUDA cores (or similar), so the occupancy isn't a big issue since we have enough parallelism. The shared memory usage is 64 floats = 256 bytes. Very small. Register count: each thread stores 8 floats, plus loop variables. Should be reasonable. Let me test this approach. I will also process multiple output positions per block to reduce overhead. Actually, let me also think about whether I can process multiple positions in one block to amortize threadblock launch overhead. If each block processes 2 or 4 output positions using more warps, the grid gets smaller and each SM can fit more blocks (but shared memory usage increases slightly). Actually, with the current design shared memory is tiny. Let me keep it simple with 1 position per block and 256 threads. Wait, I can also have fewer threads per block to allow more concurrent blocks per SM. With 256 threads/block and small shared memory, occupancy won't be limited by shared memory but by thread count. H100 supports 2048 threads per SM, so with 256 threads we can have 8 blocks per SM. 16K blocks / 132 SMs / 8 blocks = about 15 waves, which is fine. Let me compile and test.

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cpp_sources = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>

torch::Tensor fused_maxpool_sum(torch::Tensor input);
"""

cuda_sources = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <float.h>

__global__ void fused_maxpool_sum_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ input,
    float* __restrict__ output,
    int B, int C, int D1, int H1, int W1
) {
    int D1a = D1 / 2;
    int H1a = H1 / 2;
    int W1a = W1 / 2;
    int D2 = (D1a - 3) / 3 + 1;
    int H2 = (H1a - 3) / 3 + 1;
    int W2 = (W1a - 3) / 3 + 1;
    
    int total_out = B * D2 * H2 * W2;
    int pos = blockIdx.x;
    if (pos >= total_out) return;
    
    int b = pos / (D2 * H2 * W2);
    int rem = pos % (D2 * H2 * W2);
    int d2 = rem / (H2 * W2);
    rem %= (H2 * W2);
    int h2 = rem / W2;
    int w2 = rem % W2;
    
    int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32;
    int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32;
    int channels_per_warp = 8;
    int c_start = warp_id * channels_per_warp;
    
    int d_base = d2 * 6;
    int h_base = h2 * 6;
    int w_base = w2 * 6;
    
    float max_vals[8];
#pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) max_vals[i] = -FLT_MAX;
    
    size_t spatial_stride = (size_t)D1 * H1 * W1;
    
#pragma unroll
    for (int c_idx = 0; c_idx < channels_per_warp; c_idx++) {
        int c = c_start + c_idx;
        if (c >= C) break;
        size_t base_channel = ((size_t)b * C + c) * spatial_stride;
        int spatial_idx = lane_id;
        
        while (spatial_idx < 216) {
            int dc = spatial_idx / 36;
            int rem_s = spatial_idx % 36;
            int hc = rem_s / 6;
            int wc = rem_s % 6;
            
            int d = d_base + dc;
            int h = h_base + hc;
            int w = w_base + wc;
            
            float val = -FLT_MAX;
            if (d < D1 && h < H1 && w < W1) {
                val = input[base_channel + ((size_t)d * H1 + h) * W1 + w];
            }
            if (val > max_vals[c_idx]) max_vals[c_idx] = val;
            
            spatial_idx += 32;
        }
    }
    
#pragma unroll
    for (int c_idx = 0; c_idx < channels_per_warp; c_idx++) {
#pragma unroll
        for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {
            float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_vals[c_idx], offset);
            if (other > max_vals[c_idx]) max_vals[c_idx] = other;
        }
    }
    
    __shared__ float smem[64];
    if (lane_id == 0) {
#pragma unroll
        for (int c_idx = 0; c_idx < channels_per_warp; c_idx++) {
            int c = c_start + c_idx;
            if (c < C) smem[c] = max_vals[c_idx];
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    if (threadIdx.x < 32) {
        float sum = 0;
#pragma unroll
        for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += 32) {
            sum += smem[i];
        }
#pragma unroll
        for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {
            sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset);
        }
        if (threadIdx.x == 0) {
            int out_idx = ((b * 1 + 0) * D2 + d2) * H2 * W2 + h2 * W2 + w2;
            output[out_idx] = sum;
        }
    }
}

torch::Tensor fused_maxpool_sum(torch::Tensor input) {
    int B = input.size(0);
    int C = input.size(1);
    int D1 = input.size(2);
    int H1 = input.size(3);
    int W1 = input.size(4);
    
    int D1a = D1 / 2;
    int H1a = H1 / 2;
    int W1a = W1 / 2;
    int D2 = (D1a - 3) / 3 + 1;
    int H2 = (H1a - 3) / 3 + 1;
    int W2 = (W1a - 3) / 3 + 1;
    
    auto output = torch::empty({B, 1, D2, H2, W2}, input.options());
    int total_out = B * D2 * H2 * W2;
    
    int block_size = 256;
    fused_maxpool_sum_kernel<<<total_out, block_size>>>(
        input.data_ptr<float>(),
        output.data_ptr<float>(),
        B, C, D1, H1, W1
    );
    
    return output;
}
"""

module = load_inline(
    name='fused_maxpool_sum_module_v4',
    cpp_sources=cpp_sources,
    cuda_sources=cuda_sources,
    functions=['fused_maxpool_sum'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],
    verbose=False
)

fused_maxpool_sum = module.fused_maxpool_sum

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding)
    
    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)
        x = fused_maxpool_sum(x)
        return x
